Economic Special Issue Published every Monday Issue 51

Rice Monopoly and the Relationship with Food Supply Chu Yu 241

In modern society, the economy is becoming increasingly impoverished, and the food supply is out of balance. On the one hand, producers constantly fail to cover their losses due to fluctuating prices, leading to the tragic phenomenon of famine in bumper years. On the other hand, consumers are also greatly threatened in their lives due to fluctuations in supply and demand. In addition, profiteers monopolize the market. As a result of laissez-faire competition under capitalism, the structure and function of the national economy are lost. Rice is the main food of the people, and it is clear that it is not in the best interest of the country if there is no comprehensive and appropriate plan for regulation. Recently, many countries have advocated rice control and monopoly, believing that it is a fundamental policy to conserve supply and demand, maintain prices, and relieve rural areas, and it is worthy of learning from. In addition to Japan's controlled rice, which was published in this journal at the beginning of last month, the relationship between the monopoly system and the people is described below, as a reference for those who are concerned about food supply issues.

Theory of Rice Monopoly 242

On what basis is the theory of rice monopoly based? Judging from today's situation, it is an urgent matter that cannot be delayed. Here, the advantages and disadvantages of the two types of monopoly are briefly described: (1) Financial monopoly: also known as income monopoly, the intention of a country's government to implement a monopoly in order to seek financial income. This type of monopoly is mostly due to a country's financial poverty. The government purchases a certain product at a low price and resells it at a special price, using the profits from the buying and selling to supplement the government's revenue. Therefore, it can also be called a profit-making monopoly. However, if rice is monopolized and bought at a low price, farmers will inevitably suffer losses. If it is sold at a high price, consumers will bear a heavier burden. Is this not contrary to the purpose of food policy? (2) Relief monopoly: also known as charitable monopoly, it is the opposite of financial monopoly, that is, the government predetermines a certain financial loss, buying at a high price and selling at a low price. This type of monopoly is limited to special periods and is similar to our country's system of ever-normal granaries and price equalization. Buying at a high price benefits farmers, and selling at a low price benefits consumers. However, this is not always the case. For example, buying when grain is cheap during the harvest season and selling when grain prices are high before the new harvest. In countries with financial difficulties, it cannot be implemented indefinitely. (3) Economic monopoly: another new type of monopoly, different from both of the above. The government does not bear any financial burden, but also does not have any financial income. It does not return to the buying price, but adds administrative costs and sells it. The rice monopoly discussed here belongs to this type of monopoly. The government calculates the production cost of rice to buy it, adds a very small amount of expenses, and sells it to consumers. Therefore, the government has no profit or loss to speak of, and both producers and consumers are happy to comply. From this, it can be seen that the purpose of rice monopoly is definitely not to sacrifice the people or increase the government's burden because of the monopoly, nor can it be to sacrifice the government and charitably relieve the people because of the monopoly. Its purpose is to distribute important national products to the entire nation in the most reasonable way, so it is a rational socio-economic function. Therefore, the exclusive right to buy and sell must belong to the state, because private operation is not only unprofitable, but also suffers great losses. (A) The rise and fall of rice prices have a great impact on the national economy. Speculators who manipulate the market disrupt prices and attempt to obtain illegal profits everywhere. Therefore, the government, considering the entire national economy, should stabilize rice prices, that is, producers and farmers can work hard to improve crops and increase harvests due to stable prices, and consumers can also have expectations for their daily staple food due to income. (B) The economic nature of rice is that although laissez-faire free competition is allowed under capitalism, it is limited to a certain degree. To maintain the regulation of production and consumption, the entire function lies in the regulation of prices, so as to expand and contract the production and consumption of commodities. If a certain commodity is abundant, the price will rise due to increased demand, and producers will mutually seek to increase production because it is profitable. As a result, the supply of products will inevitably increase, leading to a drop in prices. The production and consumption of rice are also the same. However, although the amount of rice production can be artificially increased, it often increases or decreases due to natural and accidental reasons. It is not like other industries, which can suddenly increase production. Consumers cannot reduce their food intake due to rising prices. Although the nature of rice has now changed from domestic to international, that is, even if the country has a famine year, neighboring countries can still regulate the quantity if they are in a bumper year. But domestically, it should be monopolized by the government, which can regulate its price and quantity.

Effect of Rice Monopoly 243

The effect of rice monopoly. There are four types: (1) Monopoly can stabilize prices. (2) The monopoly system can reduce price changes by buying at a special price and selling at a low price. (3) Monopoly can establish the farmers' economy and restore the rural economy. (4) Monopoly can ensure the national food supply. However, to what extent can the above four major effects be expected? We can examine it from a pure market perspective. As mentioned above, price fluctuations are detrimental to both producers and consumers. The production and consumption of rice are different from other commodities and cannot be regulated due to price fluctuations, so it is difficult to exert its full economic function. However, the fluctuation and stability of rice prices contain various meanings: (1) Rice prices fluctuate seasonally. Generally, the price will fall a few months after the harvest, and it will be expensive during the period before the new harvest. (2) It fluctuates due to bumper and poor harvests. Generally, the price falls in bumper years and rises in poor years. (3) It fluctuates due to the economy, that is, it differs from the economic fluctuations of a grain. However, there are also times when the harvest season is normal, but the price is low, or in a year of poor harvest, the economy is quite prosperous, and rice prices are relatively high. There are also times when there is no change during the period before the new harvest because there is still a lot of rice stored from the previous year and the harvest this year is very abundant. If a monopoly is implemented, can it stabilize prices? This is discussed in the following points: (1) Seasonal fluctuations and the monopoly system: seasonal fluctuations in rice prices are most beneficial to landlords and rice merchants. Why? Because they are the owners of rice during the period before the new harvest. On the contrary, for owner-farmers, tenant farmers, and poor farmers, because they are short of money, they have to sell rice regardless of the price drop or the prospect of rising prices in the future after the rice is harvested. Especially the poor, because they are in urgent need of funds, even sell all the rice they use for their own meals. When the period before the new harvest comes, they buy it back. The loss suffered due to the buying and selling relationship is self-evident. Therefore, implementing a monopoly can alleviate seasonal price fluctuations. (2) Bumper and poor harvest movements and the monopoly system: under the monopoly system, rice prices can be fixed. However, the basic standard for determining rice prices mainly depends on the production cost of rice. This standard production cost varies depending on the bumper or poor harvest of the year. Why? Although the area of farmland cultivated by farmers and the capital and labor invested are the same every year, the resulting harvest amount increases or decreases depending on the bumper or poor harvest of the year. If a monopoly is implemented, price fluctuations due to bumper and poor harvests can be avoided. (3) Volume fluctuations and the monopoly system: rice prices often fluctuate due to the general economic climate. In times of good economy, income increases, and in times of bad economy, income decreases. Therefore, consumers can proportionally increase or decrease their staple food rice in proportion to their income, that is, in proportion to the economic fluctuations in general. Producers and farmers can increase their capital income by increasing labor demand during periods of good economy. However, because the cost of fertilizers and other raw materials is high, rice prices have to be raised. With this production cost as the standard, rice prices will naturally fluctuate according to the economy. And general prices also fluctuate due to the economic climate. If a certain price is set under the monopoly system, the trend of fluctuation can be alleviated. Because the economy of producers and farmers is more stable than the economy of a certain economy, rice price fluctuations can fluctuate below the economy. In other words, the rice index is smaller than the general price index. Although, opponents of the monopoly believe that price stability curbs hopes above a certain level. But when we compare it with the national economy as a whole, the monopoly is still beneficial. Moreover, when it comes to the problem of relieving rural areas, the current rural areas are burdened with debt and the prices of agricultural products have plummeted, which is a major problem facing us. If a monopoly is implemented to stabilize rice prices, farmers will have a certain calculation for operating agriculture, and agriculture can be expected to develop healthily. Moreover, today's rice prices are unstable, and healthy agriculture has fallen into the hands of the most dangerous speculators, resulting in the destruction of the traditional agricultural spirit. Not only that, but also because of the monopoly, the food supply can be ensured and the quantity of supply and demand can be regulated. In recent years, natural and man-made disasters have followed one after another. If private profit-making is allowed, the impact on the entire nation is unthinkable. The government can freely regulate the surplus of bumper harvests and the lack of poor harvests by storing grain; other things such as the dumping of foreign rice can also be controlled according to the monopoly law. In short, under certain conditions, the level of prices can be freely regulated and circulated in harmony.

Methods for the Government Monopoly of Rice and Grain 244

The methods of a government monopoly are the most difficult issues for a monopoly business. If the arrangements are improper, not only will the intended effects not be achieved, but endless disputes will be caused. Why? Because there must be a truly meticulous plan regarding the determination of the purchase price for the cost of production, the scope of the purchase, and the allocation for sale. Now, regarding the important methods of implementation, I will elaborate as follows: (1) Determining the purchase price issue. Those who oppose the government monopoly focus on the production cost of rice for the purchase price. However, how exactly should this so-called production cost be determined? This is truly the first point that is not easy to determine. Because the production cost of rice and grain differs depending on the location, it is vastly different between Jiangsu and Zhejiang and the two Guangdong provinces, and it is quite different between Zhili and Shandong and Gansu. To rigorously investigate the actual situation in various places is truly not easy. If purchasing based on the production cost, one would have to differentiate the price according to the land. In this way, the starting point of the monopoly system would fall into a state where implementation is impossible. However, those who advocate for a government monopoly believe that it is impossible to implement it according to the actual production cost. This is because the labor capital invested by farmers and the production cost obtained from their land cannot be bought at the same price. For example, if a farming family does not pay attention to irrigation, weeding, fertilization, pests, and diseases, they will naturally not obtain the original harvest. On the other hand, diligent and hardworking farming families often obtain above-average increases in yield. If the former is purchased at a high price and the latter is purchased at a low price, contradictions will inevitably arise. Therefore, the purchase price cannot be calculated according to the actual production cost. Instead, the actual production cost should be averaged or standardized within a certain range of production areas. However, the so-called standard production cost includes a national standard production cost and a standard production cost for a province, county, or township. What is being discussed now is in terms of the national standard. At the same time, provinces and counties should be graded separately and a compromise should be reached. For example, if the national standard production cost is 100, the entire number can be divided into twelve grades. If the index numbers for each area are 95, 96, 97, and 105, then the national production cost is 25 yuan per unit, the first grade is 23 yuan and 75 cents, the fifth grade is 24 yuan and 75 cents, and the eleventh grade is 26 yuan and 25 cents. However, calculating the national standard production cost is extremely difficult and must be calculated using technical methods. In detail, first calculate the national standard production cost according to the actual amount harvested each year. Then, multiply by the index number for each grade or location to calculate the standard production cost for each location. Based on this standard, further determine the price for each level based on the grade of rice and grain in each place. In this way, it will not be far from the actual production cost. Although there will inevitably be some difference between the actual production cost and the actual purchase price, it will be beneficial to rice and grain production, and it is also what farmers hope for. Why? Because when farmers obtain an above-average harvest with ordinary diligence, the actual production cost for each individual decreases. Deducting the purchase price allows them to obtain a reward for special labor. Conversely, if they are ordinarily lazy and cannot obtain a normal harvest, the reduction in yield results in a high actual production cost. Therefore, the government monopoly can promote the development of agriculture and the diligence and effort of farmers. (2) The scope of the purchase. This is the issue of the quantity purchased. In other words, what scope of the farmer's rice and grain production should be purchased? Those who advocate for a government monopoly are divided into two views: One is the complete purchase theory, which means purchasing all the rice and grain produced by farmers. This means that the rice and grain used by the farming family itself would be treated the same as ordinary citizens, and they would have to buy it back when they need it. The other is the partial purchase theory, which means deducting the rice and grain used by the farming family itself and purchasing the remaining surplus. From the perspective of the farming family's economy, the first view sees the major cause of poverty in rural areas today as the gradual erosion of the original nature of the farming family's economy by capitalist monetary economics. The government monopoly system can prevent this abuse. However, if all the rice and grain produced by the farming family is sold off at once, and then they have to buy it back for their own use later, the farming family's economy will inevitably be entirely monetized. This greatly contradicts the policy of rural regeneration. Therefore, the second view, the partial purchase theory, advocates deducting the rice and grain used by the farming family itself, which can prevent the monetization of rural areas. However, the amount deducted for rice and grain for personal use varies from farming family to farming family, and determining the amount is extremely difficult. Therefore, there must be a predetermined statistic for the total national consumption and the amount to be consumed per person, as well as a ratio of production and supply quantities, and family budget, etc. If the predicted amount of rice and grain consumption is not completely accurate, and there is a surplus of rice and grain due to an error in prediction, then it can be used to cover consumption in the first year. If the prediction is too low, then there is no problem. (3) The timing of the purchase. Purchasing at regular intervals is most appropriate. Ideally, the farming family should sell most of the harvested rice and grain in proportion throughout the year, except for the currency needed for annual use. However, this method not only makes government monopoly affairs complicated, but also requires a lot of distribution costs. Therefore, scholars from various countries mostly advocate for purchasing in installments. For example, purchasing three times in November and December, February and March, and June and July. However, the timing of the purchase should be decided after the purchase price is determined, and it should also be after the actual amount harvested nationwide is confirmed. The announcement of the actual amount harvested is mostly at the end of January each year in various countries. On the one hand, consider the latest harvest period and the earliest harvest period nationwide. Although there are differences in prices between the two, the government monopoly agency can make overall regulations based on the situation of the previous year. In general, when implementing a government monopoly system, the price of rice and grain is mostly not affected by seasonal fluctuations, so the price can be fixed throughout the year. (4) Purchasing agency. Under today's social conditions, rice and grain inspection agencies should be established in each province and county, directly under the central government monopoly bureau. This agency specializes in the production and consumption of rice and grain within each province, the need and supply, import and export, warehousing and out-of-warehousing matters, and reports to the central government monopoly bureau. Based on the information obtained from its report, it can be used as a reference for determining prices, and it can also be used to adjust the circulation of quantities. (5) Selling price. Now that the purchase price has been legally stipulated, the determination of the selling price can be made by adding the government monopoly business expenses to the national standard distribution cost to determine the generalized production cost. However, if the annual production cost fluctuates, the state can use technical methods to alleviate it and prevent the selling price from changing due to bumper or poor harvests. Although the production of each province, such as the quality of rice and grain, transportation, and other expenses, are very different, if the actual production cost in each province has already been investigated in detail, then it will be no different from the standard price. At the same time, in consideration of social policies, the price of the higher grade rice should be increased, and the price of the lower grade rice should be reduced in order to harmonize with the living conditions of the lower class of the people. If it is deemed necessary to provide relief rice to the poor and needy, it should be sold at a particularly low price. (6) Allocation method. It is essential to adhere to the principle of self-sufficiency in each locality. However, in various parts of the country, due to inconvenient transportation, the price of rice and grain is often determined by geographical proximity. For example, in Wuxi and Changshu counties in Jiangsu, those exported to other provinces account for 60-70%. In addition to defining self-sufficiency in terms of provinces and counties, the government monopoly system must also take into account villages and towns with less rice production and adjust excessive production and insufficient consumption. Therefore, efforts should be made to save on distribution costs and use reasonable and smooth means. However, to allocate rice and grain, it is necessary to clearly investigate the production and consumption situation in each province and county across the country, as well as be familiar with the situation of foreign rice imports, so that quantities can be circulated at any time.

The Trend of the Japanese Banking Industry by Tan Bingwen 245

The banking industry in our country, judging from Shanghai alone, appears to be booming. New banks and branch banks are not affected by the decline of other industries. If this phenomenon persists, it will become abnormal. Scholars in our country have long had insights into whether the banking system is sound and how to improve it. Japan's industrial revolution was later than that of Europe and the United States. Its national conditions are quite similar to ours, so I will introduce the current situation and trends of its banking industry for the reference of those concerned about our country's banking industry.

After the end of World War, banks in various countries have shown a tendency to concentrate. There are two steps: one is the merger of small banks by large banks, and the other is the self-merger between large-scale banks, which is different from the merger of small banks before the war. This trend also exists in Japan. Since the outbreak of the financial panic in the spring of Showa 1 (1926), the trend of bank concentration in Japan has become more intense. Due to the aftermath of the Wall Street stock market turmoil, the weaker and poorly managed banks in Japan experienced difficulties in recovering loans, reduced credit, and a flight of deposits. The functions of both lending and borrowing were hindered, and business became difficult, resulting in many bankruptcies.

Therefore, the current banking law was promulgated on January 1 of the following year, and the banking system underwent a major change. The new law stipulates that the minimum capital amount for each bank should be one million yuan or more in principle. A special provision states that "if there is a head office or branch in a specific area (referring to Tokyo and Osaka), its capital shall not be less than two million yuan." There is also a clause that states: "(In areas with a population of less than 10,000, banks that are currently operating shall increase their capital to 500,000 yuan or more within five years after the implementation of this law)." With the implementation of the new regulations, the process of mergers and acquisitions of small and medium-sized banks has become increasingly rapid. A glimpse of the situation can be seen in the report of the Ministry of Finance at the end of Showa 7 (1932), when the so-called five-year preparation period was about to expire. According to the report, before the implementation of the new banking law, the total number of ordinary banks was 1283, and 617 did not meet the statutory capital requirements, accounting for about half. Among them, 50 continued to operate after increasing their capital, while the rest were either merged or dissolved, and all disappeared within five years. During the same period, hundreds of so-called qualified banks were merged. As of January 10 of this year, there were only 538 banks in existence.

During this great elimination movement, the capital of ordinary banks also showed a very significant change. At the end of Showa 2 (1927), the total capital of 1282 such banks was 2.364 billion yuan, with an average amount of 1.843 million yuan per bank. As of January 10 of this year, the total capital of 538 banks was 1.91 billion yuan (the reasons for the reduction in the capital of ordinary banks are described below), with an average of 3.556 million yuan per bank. In other words, the number of banks decreased by 60% in five years, while the average capital per bank increased by 90%.

The main business of banks can be summarized into two aspects: receiving credit and granting credit. Receiving credit, also known as passive business, is mainly based on deposits. Granting credit, or active business, is mainly based on lending and investment. Therefore, we can also examine the progress of the Japanese banking industry from these two aspects. First, let's study the aspect of receiving credit, and list the deposit amounts of various Japanese banks at the end of Showa 1 (1926) before the financial panic and at the end of June of this year, as follows. (Unit: million Japanese yen)

Type End of Showa 1 End of June this year Remarks

Special banks 1,545 1,261 Decrease of 285

Ordinary banks 9,174 8,618 Decrease of 556

Savings banks 1,067 1,766 Increase of 699

Trust companies 5,778 6,424 Increase of 646

Postal Savings Bureau 423 1,262 Increase of 825

Others 1,199 2,756 Increase of 1,557

In the table above, the "special banks" item is set aside because its deposits are mostly government funds. However, the changes between other types of banks are very obvious: ordinary banks have decreased, while savings banks have greatly increased. The increase in trust companies and postal savings is particularly astonishing. Although some of this is due to the natural increase resulting from the development of the Japanese national economy, there is no doubt that a significant amount of funds has fled from ordinary banks to savings banks, trust companies, and postal savings.

The rapid progress of the trust business is particularly noteworthy. Japanese trust companies were established at the end of Taisho 12 (1923). As of the end of June of this year, about 11 years had passed, and the capital had reached 6,414,000,000 yen. According to the Japanese Ministry of Communications' observation, the increase in postal savings mainly came from current deposits in banks (mainly ordinary banks), reaching 700-800 million yen. The increase in savings bank deposits is because these banks are affiliated with relatively large banks and have strong capital systems, so their credit is quite solid, and they have been able to erode the deposits of ordinary banks.

The reduction in special bank deposits is due to the expansion of the Japanese government's finances. The sharp decline in ordinary bank deposits, in addition to the competition from other types of banks mentioned above, the prosperity of speculative businesses is an important factor. In this situation, people believe that bank interest rates are too low and invest in public bonds, corporate bonds, real estate stocks, and other transactions. The amount of funds that have fled as a result must be no less than the amount that has fled to savings banks and other banks.

Every organization has its economic background. The changes in Japanese banks are a reflection of its national economy. For example, the development of the trust business is the result of the increase in large deposits from the domestic asset class. As for the inflow of funds into postal savings and savings banks, it is mostly small deposits from the public. In the past, because special banks were small in scale or had not yet been established, both large and small deposits were deposited in ordinary banks. However, as the economic situation progresses, banks that meet new needs will inevitably emerge, so funds will flow from old organizations to their appropriate places. This is similar to the fact that the class boundaries are not clear in countries where the industry is underdeveloped. However, as the industry becomes more developed, the class divisions become more distant. Therefore, we can predict that if the Japanese economy does not collapse, this phenomenon of fund differentiation will tend to be more intense in the future.

In the situation described above, not only do ordinary banks experience changes in deposits due to external relations, but changes also occur among ordinary banks themselves. That is, deposits are shifting from banks with smaller capital to banks with stronger capital. Large-scale ordinary banks mostly have head offices in large cities and branches throughout the country, making it easier to carry out their lending, investment, deposit, and other businesses.

Next, let's look at the bank's credit granting business. There are also significant changes. Whether in lending or investment, large transactions are becoming increasingly popular. This trend is particularly evident in the business of large banks. Although the number of small transactions is also increasing, the average amount per transaction is decreasing. These two trends are closely related to the changes and concentration of funds mentioned above. However, the decisive factor is the so-called large-scale demand for funds. The reasons for this large-scale demand are the progress and control of the business world, as well as the increase in public bonds due to financial expansion.

It is beneficial for the banking industry to use large-scale income to finance large-scale businesses. This not only saves various cumbersome procedures and operating expenses, but also because large-scale loans or investments have greater certainty (it is easier to investigate the object of financing and the content of its business). Therefore, these large banks with excess funds should use them for large transactions, whether from a theoretical or practical point of view. As for small and medium-sized banks, because their capital is weak, they cannot invest in large amounts to compete with large banks, so they have to work hard on a wide range of small transactions. However, the results are not satisfactory: the number of transactions increases, but the content decreases. This differentiation of bank credit granting business will inevitably cause the differentiation of deposits at the same time.

The degree of the world economic panic is deepening, the trend of financial expansion in various countries is becoming more intense, and the adoption of so-called cartel trust monopoly businesses and control policies is becoming more widespread. Both public and private businesses need large amounts of money. As a result, large banks give full play to their abilities, while small and medium-sized banks are in a position where they cannot cope. Small banks have to actively attempt mergers (Absorption) in order to find a way out. Even large banks are competing for large and high-quality loans and investments, and resorting to the law of survival of the fittest, implementing joint-stock personnel mergers.

Bank mergers in Europe and the United States have been absolutely prevalent for half a century. As for mergers or combinations (Amalgamation) between large banks, they have only become popular since the great war. (Please refer to "On Bank Mergers" in Bank Weekly, Volume 17, Issue 21.) The progress in Japan is no different. The difference is that the banking system of a country must have a special economic foundation. For example, the industrial revolution in the United Kingdom occurred the earliest, so the evolution (merger process) of the banking system was also the earliest. Therefore, by 1925, the capital of the "Big Five Banks" accounted for about 70% of the national bank capital. The degree of bank merger in Japan is far inferior (see the table on the left), because the majority of small and medium-sized industries and agriculture in Japan still occupy a relatively important position. However, Japan's journey towards bank concentration is also very rapid. As mentioned above, at the end of Showa 1 (1926), there were 1283 banks in Japan. By the beginning of this year, the number had decreased to 538. The recent merger (joint-stock) of the 34th, Yamaguchi, and Konoike banks has broken the record of the Japanese banking industry. The following is a list of the national bank survey by the Ministry of Finance at the end of January this year.

Bank type Number of banks Total capital amount (unit: thousand yuan)

Below 1 million yuan 147 79,78

Below 2 million yuan 214 238,857

Below 5 million yuan 94 249,535

Below 10 million yuan 38 241,967

Below 30 million yuan 37 486,879

Below 50 million yuan 1 33,988

Above 50 million yuan 7 579,700

Total 538 1,910,461

The fourteen large banks with capital of 50 million yuan or more mentioned on the right have recently merged with Yamaguchi, so it has actually become a bank. Although the remaining capital only accounts for 30% of the total capital of ordinary banks, if the basis is 10 million yuan, there are still 493 small and medium-sized banks below that, accounting for more than 90% of the number of ordinary banks. Since the capital of these banks is so weak, it is obvious that they are not suitable for the needs of the new environment. Now that the United States and Russia have resumed diplomatic relations, the international situation is quite unfavorable to Japan. The Japanese government is bound to expand its finances again to expand its military preparations as a precaution. The internal control business in Japan is also expanding more and more. All in all, there is a need for large amounts of money. In other words, the investment places for those with strong financial resources in the Japanese financial world are expanding more and more, while those with weak financial resources are feeling more and more difficult. In this situation, it can be predicted that it will follow in the footsteps of the United Kingdom.

Concerning the Highly Dreadful Bond Market of Last Week: Conflicting Information, Rampant Rumors, Overall Drops Ranging from One or Two Yuan to Three or Five Yuan 249

Last week, from November 27th to December 2nd, the Chinese Securities Exchange, since the Fujian Incident, had already moved into its second act. Furthermore, due to the November futures settlement, market prices experienced even greater fluctuations. One wave after another, news changed by the hour, and rumors abounded. Sometimes the situation in Fujian and Zhejiang was described as perilous, other times Guangdong and Fujian were said to have reached an understanding. There were calls to welcome Hu Hanmin, then to persuade Cai Tingkai. Guangdong's attitude shifted, sometimes appearing obedient to the central government, sometimes allied with Fujian, and sometimes remaining an observer. With war seemingly imminent, mediation efforts continued ceaselessly. The situation was bizarre and bewildering, yet these were merely peripheral issues, not the crux of the matter. The bond market fluctuated wildly, demonstrating the full extent of its volatility. The situation grew increasingly chaotic. Major players maintained control, their actions seemingly based on genuine holdings rather than speculative manipulations to create an illusion of prosperity. On Monday, with the November futures having concluded, the market focused solely on December. The Fujian Incident news subsided, and both buyers and sellers adopted a wait-and-see approach. Market prices remained relatively stable, with only minor fluctuations. On Tuesday, the situation in Fujian and Zhejiang intensified, with war clouds gathering and Guangdong remaining on the sidelines. Market prices fell, with most bonds reaching new lows, dropping another one or two yuan. On Wednesday, the November futures settlement was processed with little market activity. On Thursday, rumors were rampant, with reports of fighting breaking out in Zhejiang and Fujian, and even rumors of a "Third Government" being established. Public anxiety was palpable. Major players sold off their holdings, causing various bonds to hit new lows across the board. The "Residual Troops" and the "First Customs" bonds even triggered a trading halt after falling over four yuan in the afternoon, marking the second major crash of the year. January futures were launched and immediately saw widespread short selling. On Friday, the market was extremely chaotic, with large swings that were difficult to predict. Only two trading sessions took place throughout the day. Guangdong advocated for the Fujian side to dissolve its new organization and seek alternative solutions to the current situation, further fueling public anxiety. Prices generally fell in the morning, but in the afternoon, southwest mediation efforts led to a strong rebound, with prices surging one or two yuan. As a result, prices ended up rising one or two yuan overall. On Saturday, prices initially continued to rise, but after the second session, news of disrupted telegram lines in Wenzhou and the submission of five conditions by the southwest caused prices to fall again. The market remained highly chaotic, but the outcome was relatively positive, with a slight rise of a few jiao. With the prospect of war looming, the future remains uncertain. As a result of last week's trading, prices fell by amounts ranging from one or two yuan to two or five yuan, with spot prices falling by as much as six yuan. The total transaction volume was 12,045,460 yuan. The weekly results, including highs, lows, and price changes, are summarized in the table below (unit: yuan, trading unit: 100 yuan):

Grain and Rice Market Trends Stabilized 251

Yesterday, the sources of goods in Southern Shanghai were similar. There were over 30 lots of late rice (5,000+ shi), and nearly 20 lots of thick japonica rice of various grades (4,000+ shi). After the late rice market situation had been able to stabilize slightly the day before yesterday, sellers immediately called for higher prices upon opening, and as the Qingdao and Yantai gangs were not affected by the weekend and began to sell, the market gained momentum. Chang Gang Special Special: 7.80, Pingwang Yin Yuan: 6.30, Shipu Late Package: 6.63

Free Talk

On Change 252

— Qu Yi

The Book of Changes says: "Closing the door is called Kun, opening the door is called Qian; opening and closing, this is called change. Coming and going without end, this is called flowing." It also says: "When things reach their limit, they change; when they change, they flow." Therefore, what is called change is to seek flow. Hollowing out wood to make boats, shaping wood to make oars; boats and oars, these are changes in wood. The benefit of boats and oars is to cross the impassable, reaching far and benefiting the world. In ancient times, people lived in caves and in the wild; later sages "changed" this with palaces and houses; and today's modern men and women have places to dance their waltzes. In ancient times, people used knotted cords to govern; later generations "changed" this with written contracts, so officials could govern and the people could be examined; and today's literati can use these to wage ink-and-paper lawsuits. Change is transformation. Change and transformation are both used to seek flow. However, the change of the ancients necessarily sought to understand the emotions of heaven, earth, all things, yin, yang, cold, and heat. The change of today's people necessarily seeks to conform to the historical and natural laws. The words are different, but the meaning is the same.

There is a person who closes his door to read, steeping himself in the classics, never tiring. He always considers the noise outside the door to be vulgar and not to be approached. One day, the wind blows the door open, and he steps outside. He realizes that what his eyes see and his hands touch are worth more. He then understands that understanding human affairs is the essence of writing. He suddenly realizes what makes a person human, and unconsciously his hands dance and his feet step. And I, in my small-mindedness, scold him, saying: "You used to despise the noise, but now you dance in the midst of it. Your change is shameless!" This is also to see little and be surprised by much, like seeing a camel and calling it a horse with a swollen back, completely lacking understanding of the principle of change.

Zhuangzi said: "Seeds have different potentials. When they get water, they become 'ji'; when they get water and soil, they become frogspawn clothing; when they grow on mounds and dunes, they become lingxi." The specific things named are not important, but the meaning is very valuable. For things inevitably change their growth according to the suitability of the water and soil. From egg to larva to pupa to adult insect, this is the growth process of insects. Anyone with basic scientific knowledge knows this. Yet its changes can be said to be numerous. And I again, fixated on the larva and adult insect, scold it, saying: "You used to be an egg, but now you appear as a larva; you used to be a pupa, but now you appear as an adult insect." Wouldn't I be laughed at by the insects and their descendants?

Yet words are the voice of the heart. Where words reach, the heart stops. The Book of Songs says: "The green caterpillar has offspring, and the guo lu carries it away." Zheng Xuan's commentary says: "The pulu (guo lu) takes the offspring of the mulberry worm, carries them away, warms and nurtures them to make them its own offspring." Tao Hongjing's commentary on the Herbal Classic and Cheng Yaotian's "Small Record of Insects" both refute this, saying that the guo lu is a type of wasp that builds mud chambers on the eaves, lays its eggs inside, and catches green caterpillars (the so-called green caterpillar) to feed them. If the "offspring of the guo lu" are fed and grow, eventually transforming into guo lu, this natural change is not strange. Yet the poet must refer to them as the offspring of the green caterpillar. Yang Xiong's "Fa Yan" then adds to this, saying: "The offspring of the green caterpillar die, and when they encounter the guo lu, they pray: 'Be like me! Be like me!' After a long time, they resemble it." Then, those who do not believe that the "offspring of the guo lu" transform into guo lu, do they have the lingering regret of Yang Xiong? Saying that they are not like me.

I am moved by the fact that people today use "changeable" to scold people, and do not use historical and natural laws to measure how the change is. I write "On Change."

A Quiet Record of Fangyan 253

— Yu Dafu

Fangyan is located fifty li northeast of Yongkang County. From Jinhua to Yongkang is about a hundred li, and there are public buses available. However, from Yongkang to Fangyan, one must either take a sedan chair or walk. On the day we went, because the sky was overcast and threatening rain, we all took sedan chairs after getting off the public bus in Yongkang and proceeded east. After fifteen li, we passed Jinshan Village. Another fifteen li brought us to Zhiying, a large town with about a thousand households, mostly surnamed Ying. We stopped the sedan chairs for a brief rest. The rain grew heavier, so we bought some oiled paper and the like to serve as rain gear. After traveling another ten or so li, mountains began to rise on both sides. The peaks and rocks were peculiar, and old trees grew haphazardly. Viewed in the light rain, they took on various shapes. The sedan bearers pointed them out one by one, saying: "This is Grandma Rock, that is Tiger Rock... Rat Ladder," and so on, rattling off a long list. After a few more li, we arrived at Yanxia Street, already at the foot of Fangyan.

Anyone who has been to Jinhua should have this experience. After checking into a hotel, some country gentlemen in dark blue long gowns, refined and courteous, will come to question you: "Are you going to Fangyan to burn incense? How many times have you come to offer incense? Which inn have you stayed at before?" If you answer that it is your first time going to Fangyan, they will hand you a business card and ask you to stay at their inn when you go up to Fangyan. These are all "innkeepers" from Yanxia Street, serving as customer greeters who are like hotels but slightly different. Hundreds of li away, these agents are dispatched to solicit business, which shows both the popularity of the Fangyan incense market throughout the year and the fierce competition among the four or five hundred families in Yanxia Street.

The so-called "innkeepers" of Yanxia Street, who run the hotel business and rely entirely on the Hugong Temple for their livelihood, number three to five thousand in total. Most are surnamed Cheng or Ying, and their literary style is extremely prosperous, and their property is also considerable. The houses are all three-story buildings. Generally, the lower level is built in the valley, the middle level is along the street, and the upper level is a building. Each inn has thirty to fifty rooms, and during the peak incense season, it is said that every inn is overcrowded. Pilgrims come from Shaoxing, Chuzhou, Hangzhou, and nearby counties, and there are also those who come from as far away as Fujian.

From Yanxia Street, after another three or five li of winding roads, one ascends the mountain. The stone steps on the mountain are countless, dense and steep, winding and circling. It takes an hour to reach the Fengmen of the Hugong Temple.

Hugong's given name was Ze, his courtesy name was Zizheng. He was from Yongkang and served as the Vice Minister of War during the Song Dynasty. He often petitioned for the exemption of the Dingqian tax for the people of Quzhou, Muzhou, and Wuzhou. Therefore, the people were grateful and established a temple to worship him. Hugong studied at Fangyan when he was young, so the temple in Fangyan is considered the genuine and oldest one. Moreover, he often performed miracles. The most notable of these are the following:

During the reign of Emperor Huizong of the Song Dynasty, bandits invaded Yongkang. The villagers took refuge in Fangyan. Fangyan had a "Thousand People Pit" with large vines hanging down. When the bandits arrived, they climbed up the vines, but suddenly a red snake was seen biting through the vines, and all the bandits fell to their deaths.

Bandits arose in Qingxi and occupied Fangyan. The leader of the bandits dreamed one night that the god was watering his horse in the pool of the rock, and at dawn the pool was dry, and his followers were terrified and dispersed.

During the Taiping Rebellion, nearby villages were often looted, but Fangyan remained unscathed.

In the third year of the Republic of China, villagers from Sheng County, admiring the spiritual power of Hugong, built a temple to worship him. Under cover of darkness, they came to Fangyan to steal Hugong's head, intending to use it to make an idol. Hugong appeared in a dream to the leaders and farmers of nearby villages, instructing them to arrest the thieves who stole the idol's head. All the thieves were soon arrested. In the winter of that year, a large fire broke out in a village in Sheng County, and all those who had heard about the theft of Hugong's head lost their homes. The following August, two villagers from that village came to offer incense, and both died on the road.

Similar miracles and supernatural events are countless, so the incense fire at Fangyan burns continuously throughout the year, especially during the spring and autumn seasons. Pilgrims come to worship from all directions, hundreds of li away. The people of Jinhua who travel far from their hometowns build Hugong Temples in their respective locations to worship him. Although it is said to be superstition, the extensive influence of his transformation and power is truly beyond our expectations. This is a brief explanation of why Fangyan's reputation can spread far and wide. As for our reason for traveling thousands of miles to see Fangyan for ourselves, it lies in the mountain's secluded, spiritual beauty, which is completely different from other mountains. "To be continued"

Fishing 254

— Sheng Huanming

The impressions left from childhood, those that are more clearly engraved in my mind, are mostly from the summer. The sweltering heat that makes one's whole body covered with prickly heat, the unbearable itching of poisonous mosquito bites that fill all ten fingernails with dirt, are not easily forgotten.

At night, everyone sits on the scorching stone steps or doorsteps to cool off. Mosquitoes hum incessantly, and the fire of the water pipe flickers on and off in the darkness. Far away, the summer evening breeze gently carries the scent of rice paddies and the creaking sound of the waterwheel. While others chat, I sit alone, scratching my red bumps, listening intently. The conversations are mostly about stories of the Taiping rebels "searching the mountains", "places to cultivate immortality", or the appearance of zombies, which make people both disgusted and afraid. The heat inside the house has not yet dissipated, and as soon as you cross the threshold, you can hear the buzzing of mosquitoes. Mosquitoes attack everywhere, whether it is your nose, ears, or mouth. If a child goes alone towards the lake, people will say:

"Ming Ge'er, do you see that? Under the willow tree, there's a water ghost with red hair!"

And I would be frightened back.

But summer nights don't always give me a sense of unease. As soon as someone says "Let's go fishing!", I would become excited.

However, fishing doesn't happen often. There are two obstacles: the lake outside the door rarely "divides the well" even in years with little rain (the elders say that there are some places with no bottom); the "seed fish" released into the lake at the beginning of the year would at most weigh only three or four jin by then. It would be a pity to catch them all, because there would be no big fish to net in the winter. This is not allowed.

But it doesn't mean that there is no fishing in the summer. It does happen: This usually takes place on moonlit nights. The sound of gongs rings out, and people with "pestles" (note) wade into the Bailing Lake in groups, catching fish noisily until past midnight.

During these rare fishing trips, the elders invariably express their dissatisfaction:

"Look at this 'world'! It 'must change!'" The squinting Uncle Gui grumbles: "It must change. Instead of taking the fish to the city to sell, they... In the old days, fishing was not allowed until winter. This world!"

"Damn old thing!" I mutter a fish head in my mouth, hiding behind the door, thinking: "When these old things die, then we can go fishing every day!"

Last summer, I returned to my hometown and met him by the lake. He had aged beyond recognition. He tapped his water pipe against an old willow tree, his lips trembling: "I'm old, useless, these things!" He rubbed his large, gnarled hands, roughened by labor, and stared blankly at the fishermen.

Whether on the lake's edge, on rafts, or in the shallow water, people cast their nets or use other fishing gear to catch fish, relying on their luck. The ripples of fishing in Bailing Lake collide with each other. The lake water has all turned a deathly color.

"In the old days - sigh, not even the Taiping rebels took as much as this. You intellectuals understand, is there anything in the books that says you can fish like this in the summer? - There isn't. It's changed!"

Soon, I understood: In the evening, or when the sun was about to set, I saw them carrying the fish they had caught in wooden basins or stringing them through their gills with willow branches and going to the city. When they returned, they brought back some necessities that even the poor couldn't do without. Bailing Lake was clearly their only source of emergency funds.

When I left my hometown, it was winter. It was clear that Bailing Lake had been fished out.

"We should pool some money to buy 'seed fish'!" someone said. Everyone was silent.

Note: "Pestle" is a local term. A type of bamboo-woven fishing tool, about three feet tall and circular. It is inserted into the bottom of the water, and if a fish is detected, a hand is reached down from above to catch it.

Two Brothers (48) 255

— Guy de Maupassant, translated by Li Weike

But suddenly, as if awakening from a dream, he saw them clearly; and because they seemed happy and content, he suddenly felt a resentment toward them.

Now, as he brushed past the groups of people, circling around them, he became absorbed in new thoughts. The multicolored decorations covering the beach like bouquets of flowers, the beautiful fabrics, the dazzling parasols, the unnatural coquetry of unfree bodies, the ingenious inventions of all the fashions, from the exquisite shoes to the strange hats, the seductive postures, voices, and smiles, in short, all the flamboyant displays on this beach, suddenly seemed to him like a field of blooming flowers of female vice. All these women, so elaborately adorned, wanted to please, seduce, and attract someone. They were for men, all men! Except for the husbands who no longer needed to be conquered — they were adorning themselves. They were adorning themselves for the lovers of today or tomorrow, for the strangers they might meet, notice, or anticipate.

And these men, sitting near them, looking into their eyes, talking to them with their lips so close, calling out to them, wanting to have them, though it seemed so near and easy, yet they chased them like hunting a nimble and cunning bird or beast. Therefore, this vast beach was nothing more than a market of love, where some women sold themselves, and others gave themselves away, where some women priced their caresses, and others offered themselves. All these women were thinking only of the same thing, presenting and making others envy their bodies, which had been given, sold, or promised to other men. And he thought that this was the same everywhere on the whole earth.

His mother had done the same thing as other women! Like other women — no! There were also exceptions, and many, many! Now, he was seeing wealthy, fervent, love-seeking women, who probably all belonged to that elegant and frivolous society, or even to the society of prostitution, because on this beach, trampled by idlers, one would not encounter the virtuous women who stayed deeply within their homes.

The tide rose, slowly driving the first rows of bathers back toward the city. The groups of people, seeing the yellow wave crested with a ring of foam surge up, hastily stood up, grabbing their chairs and fleeing. The small changing houses on wheels, drawn by a horse, also moved up; and the boardwalk, paved with planks and running along the beach from one end to the other, was now filled with people, black and slow-moving, some going east, some going west, shoulder to shoulder, heel to toe, mingling together. The hypersensitive Pierre, becoming agitated by brushing against them, fled them, running alone into the city, stopping at the door of a simple hotel, and going in for lunch.

After he had eaten and drunk his coffee, he lay back on two chairs outside the door, for he had not slept at all that night, and he dozed off under a banyan tree.

After resting for several hours, when he awoke, it was already time to hurry back to catch the boat. So, he took the road to the pier, troubled all the way by the fatigue that had quickly descended upon him during his nap. Now, he wanted to return, wanted to know if his mother had found Maraisa's portrait. Would she bring it up first, or would he have to ask again? If she was still waiting for someone else to ask, then she must have a secret reason for not wanting to show the portrait to anyone.

Talks

Love, Money Worship, and Hedonism 256

— Qiong Sheng

Now that the freedom of love in China has moved from discussion to widespread reality, few people pay attention. However, this does not mean that the "true meaning" of the freedom of love has been thoroughly understood and consistently practiced. The reason for the silence is that two extreme viewpoints are dominating people's hearts, failing to "grasp" the relationship between sex and love, and between material things and the spirit of both sexes. This has led to the current widespread phenomenon of romantic, carnal pleasure, and the common occurrence of civil lawsuits such as "seducing underage girls" and abandoning them without providing support.

Both of these phenomena are manifested against the backdrop of the current economic bankruptcy. To fundamentally solve this, it is natural to start by changing the current economic situation and the social structure that caused the economic bankruptcy. Without a correct understanding of the relationship between sex and love, spirit and material things, and without doing one's "best" within this understanding, it cannot be denied that the relationship between the sexes is becoming increasingly worse. The reason for not correctly understanding the relationship between sex and love, spirit and material things, is the pure money worship in the relationship between the sexes, and the simple mechanism that regards sexual desire as the basis of all relationships between the sexes.

Shortly after the "May Fourth" movement, which represented the national independence movement and democratic revolution, the terms freedom of love, freedom of divorce, and freedom of marriage were introduced. At that time, although China was also suffering from imperialist aggression, the social and economic bankruptcy was far less severe than it is now. Therefore, a group of intellectuals whose economic status was neither high nor low once advocated "sacred love." However, in recent years, China's economy has been greatly bankrupted under the influence of the global economic panic and the intensified plunder by various powers. The men and women of this group of declining small and medium-sized classes have turned to the money worship of love, and to the mechanism that takes sexual desire as the center and equates love with sexual desire. The former, in their conduct and experience, regards money as the greatest magic power, and disregards emotions, selling their bodies whole or in parts as soon as they see money. The latter takes sexual desire as everything, attempting to temporarily numb themselves to reality and engaging in romance at will. The former is obsessed with being the plaything of financial tycoons, wealthy wives, and young ladies, while the latter "lives for the day," hoping to forget their worries through pleasure. However, both are reflections of the decline of the middle class. Of course, a small number of people have their luck and become wives or relatives of officials, but most of them are discarded like worn-out shoes after several attempts. Even if they appear in court, they only add a few more appearance fees for the lawyers. There are even many who become wronged ghosts at the Huangpu River or the protagonists of suicides at the Hua'an Building. As for the advocates of pure sexual pleasure, regardless of temporary or short-term "carelessness," they not only cannot forget reality after intoxication, but also inevitably experience spiritual pain because people are emotional beings of flesh and blood. In the current economic bankruptcy, the emergence of love money worship is naturally a manifestation of the middle class surrendering to the enemy. Under the view of "you have money, I have beauty," the practice of selling the body is undoubtedly harmful to the correct development of the relationship between the sexes. However, the enjoyment of carnal pleasure is also far from a progressive consciousness. Celebrities quote materialistic views to defend it, believing that "love is sexual desire" is a materialistic understanding. The harm it causes is no less than surrendering to the enemy. In addition to the vulgar view of mechanically using materialism to prove and distinguish human relationships, it also directly promotes the harmful phenomenon of indulging in carnal pleasure and avoiding reality. We criticize love money worship. It is only natural that some people with weak will lose their bodies and minds under the temptation of money in the current environment where money can do anything, and there is nothing to discuss. The fundamental problem lies in the fact that money is concentrated in the hands of a few people, and the "freedom of love" is inevitably used as a morbid phenomenon of indulging in desire.

Therefore, while we do not agree with love money worship or the enjoyment of carnal pleasure, we should see them as economic and social phenomena. People with no resources who cannot afford to take their lovers to dance halls and movie theaters do not need to "mourn like bereaved parents" over their broken hearts. They should just think that they have seen the expensive "Southern ham" at Sincere Company and dare not ask about it, and they will feel relieved. Seeing the phenomenon of enjoying pleasure by going to hotels and dance halls, there is no need to lament that "the world is going downhill and people's hearts are not what they used to be," because it will also decline with the progress of the times.

Some people say that in this day and age, old women and young girls compete for extravagance and evil, and the freedom of love leads to promiscuity and chaos. In fact, these are all extreme views. The phenomenon of seeing money and opening their eyes, and not caring about selling their bodies, and the fact of being intoxicated in pleasure without knowing the affairs of the world, are inevitable in their occurrence and inevitable in their extinction. Moralists cannot do anything by concerning themselves with social customs and shouting loudly.

Secrets to Asking for Help 257

— You Yi

The other day I saw an article by Mr. (Shen Min) entitled "Notes on Visiting Friends," which referred to the methods that must be known when visiting important people: it was also very pleasant, but since it specifically referred to visiting important people, the word "friend" in visiting friends seemed to be not very appropriate, because important people naturally everyone wants to know them and be friends with them: However, they may not be willing to lightly recognize you as a friend. This word "friend" may not be established without the other party's permission; even if you knew him before he was promoted and made a fortune, and were very familiar with him, and he stubbornly cannot get rid of you, I guess his respectful title for you will at least be limited to work, and he will also add the word "poor" to the word "friend": He will say to everyone, "He is a poor friend from the past." Listen to that, this word "poor" is so embarrassing, it is better to cancel this title and be done with it: Therefore, I changed this topic to "Secrets to Asking for Help" and supplemented a few more sentences. Fortunately, in today's society, except for those who carry long guns and talk about hard fighting, and those who carry pistols and talk about hard robbery, no one can survive in the world without asking for help. Secrets to asking for help must be known. In my opinion, it is also very easy, nothing more than "boasting" and "flattering." First, start with the subject of conversation: If he likes prostitutes, talk to him about prostitution; if he likes gambling, talk to him about gambling; if he doesn't like to talk about this, and wants to study knowledge and value talents to gain fame and reputation, then you can pretend that you have returned from studying in the East and West, and that you have specialized knowledge, expertise, and superb skills, and flatter him as a great politician, great economist, and great educator. As long as you boast and flatter appropriately, or add some nighttime entertainment, you will definitely win the heart of the important person and get an official position.
